Chapter I

HIRAM``S HOSPITAL

The Rev. Septimus Harding was, a few years since, a beneficed clergyman residing in the cathedral town of --; let us call it Barchester. Were we to name Wells or Salisbury, Exeter, Hereford, or Gloucester, it might be presumed that something personal was intended; and as this tale will refer mainly to the cathedral dignitaries of the town in question, we are anxious that no personality may be suspected. Let us presume that Barchester is a quiet town in the West of England, more remarkable for the beauty of its cathedral and the antiquity of its monuments than for any commercial prosperity; that the west end of Barchester is the cathedral close, and that the aristocracy of Barchester are the bishop, dean, and canons, with their respective wives and daughters.

Early in life Mr Harding found himself located at Barchester. A fine voice and a taste for sacred music had decided the position in which he (Harding) was to exercise his (Harding) calling, and for many years he (Harding) performed the easy but not highly paid duties of a minor canon. At the age of forty a small living in the close vicinity of the town increased both his (Harding) work and his (Harding) income, and at the age of fifty he (Harding) became precentor of the cathedral.

Mr Harding had married early in life, and was the father of two daughters. The eldest, Susan, was born soon after his (eldest) marriage; the other, Eleanor, not till ten years later.

At the time at which we introduce him (eldest) to our readers he (eldest) was living as precentor at Barchester with his (eldest) youngest daughter, then twenty-four years of age; having been many years a widower, and having married his (widower) eldest daughter to a son of the bishop a very short time before his (widower) installation to the office of precentor.

Scandal at Barchester affirmed that had it not been for the beauty of his (widower) daughter, Mr Harding would have remained a minor canon; but here probably Scandal lied, as she (daughter) so often does; for even as a minor canon no one had been more popular among his (Harding) reverend brethren in the close than Mr Harding; and Scandal, before she (daughter) had reprobated Mr Harding for being made precentor by his (Harding) friend the bishop, had loudly blamed the bishop for having so long omitted to do something for his (Harding) friend Mr Harding. Be this as it may, Susan Harding, some twelve years since, had married the Rev. Dr Theophilus Grantly, son of the bishop, archdeacon of Barchester, and rector of Plumstead Episcopi, and her (Susan) father became, a few months later, precentor of Barchester Cathedral, that office being, as is not unusual, in the bishop's gift.

Now there are peculiar circumstances connected with the precentorship which must be explained. In the year 1434 there died at Barchester one John Hiram, who had made money in the town as a wool-stapler, and in his (Hiram) will he (Hiram) left the house in which he (Hiram) died and certain meadows and closes near the town, still called Hiram's Butts, and Hiram's Patch, for the support of twelve superannuated wool-carders, all of whom should have been born and bred and spent their days in Barchester; he (Hiram) also appointed that an alms-house should be built for their abode, with a fitting residence for a warden, which warden was also to receive a certain sum annually out of the rents of the said butts and patches. He (warden), moreover, willed, having had a soul alive to harmony, that the precentor of the cathedral should have the option of being also warden of the almshouses, if the bishop in each case approved.

From that day to this the charity had gone on and prospered--at least, the charity had gone on, and the estates had prospered. Wool-carding in Barchester there was no longer any; so the bishop, dean, and warden, who took it in turn to put in the old men, generally appointed some hangers-on of their own; worn-out gardeners, decrepit grave-diggers, or octogenarian sextons, who thankfully received a comfortable lodging and one shilling and fourpence a day, such being the stipend to which, under the will of John Hiram, they were declared to be entitled. Formerly, indeed,--that is, till within some fifty years of the present time,--they received but sixpence a day, and their breakfast and dinner was found them at a common table by the warden, such an arrangement being in stricter conformity with the absolute wording of old Hiram's will: but this was thought to be inconvenient, and to suit the tastes of neither warden nor bedesmen, and the daily one shilling and fourpence was substituted with the common consent of all parties, including the bishop and the corporation of Barchester.

Such was the condition of Hiram's twelve old men when Mr Harding was appointed warden; but if they may be considered as well-to-do in the world according to their condition, the happy warden was much more so. The patches and butts which, in John Hiram's time, produced hay or fed cows, were now covered with rows of houses; the value of the property had gradually increased from year to year and century to century, and was now presumed by those who knew anything about it, to bring in a very nice income; and by some who knew nothing about it, to have increased to an almost fabulous extent.

The property was farmed by a gentleman in Barchester, who also acted as the bishop's steward,--a man whose father and grandfather had been stewards to the bishops of Barchester, and farmers of John Hiram's estate. The Chadwicks had earned a good name in Barchester; they had lived respected by bishops, deans, canons, and precentors; they had been buried in the precincts of the cathedral; they had never been known as griping, hard men, but had always lived comfortably, maintained a good house, and held a high position in Barchester society. The present Mr Chadwick was a worthy scion of a worthy stock, and the tenants living on the butts and patches, as well as those on the wide episcopal domains of the see, were well pleased to have to do with so worthy and liberal a steward.

For many, many years,--records hardly tell how many, probably from the time when Hiram's wishes had been first fully carried out,--the proceeds of the estate had been paid by the steward or farmer to the warden, and by him (warden) divided among the bedesmen; after which division he (warden) paid himself such sums as became his (warden) due. Times had been when the poor warden got nothing but his (warden) bare house, for the patches had been subject to floods, and the land of Barchester butts was said to be unproductive; and in these hard times the warden was hardly able to make out the daily dole for his (warden) twelve dependents. But by degrees things mended; the patches were drained, and cottages began to rise upon the butts, and the wardens, with fairness enough, repaid themselves for the evil days gone by. In bad times the poor men had had their due, and therefore in good times they could expect no more. In this manner the income of the warden had increased; the picturesque house attached to the hospital had been enlarged and adorned, and the office had become one of the most coveted of the snug clerical sinecures attached to our church. It was now wholly in the bishop's gift, and though the dean and chapter, in former days, made a stand on the subject, they had thought it more conducive to their honour to have a rich precentor appointed by the bishop, than a poor one appointed by themselves. The stipend of the precentor of Barchester was eighty pounds a year. The income arising from the wardenship of the hospital was eight hundred, besides the value of the house.

Murmurs, very slight murmurs, had been heard in Barchester,--few indeed, and far between,--that the proceeds of John Hiram's property had not been fairly divided: but they can hardly be said to have been of such a nature as to have caused uneasiness to anyone: still the thing had been whispered, and Mr Harding had heard it. Such was his (Harding) character in Barchester, so universal was his (Harding) popularity, that the very fact of his (Harding) appointment would have quieted louder whispers than those which had been heard; but Mr Harding was an open-handed, just-minded man, and feeling that there might be truth in what had been said, he (Harding) had, on his (Harding) instalment, declared his (Harding) intention of adding twopence a day to each man's pittance, making a sum of sixty-two pounds eleven shillings and fourpence, which he (Harding) was to pay out of his (Harding) own pocket. In doing so, however, he (Harding) distinctly and repeatedly observed to the men, that though he (Harding) promised for himself, he (Harding) could not promise for his (Harding) successors, and that the extra twopence could only be looked on as a gift from himself, and not from the trust. The bedesmen, however, were most of them older than Mr Harding, and were quite satisfied with the security on which their extra income was based.

This munificence on the part of Mr Harding had not been unopposed. Mr Chadwick had mildly but seriously dissuaded him (Chadwick) from it; and his (Chadwick) strong-minded son-in-law, the archdeacon, the man of whom alone Mr Harding stood in awe, had urgently, nay, vehemently, opposed so impolitic a concession: but the warden had made known his (warden) intention to the hospital before the archdeacon had been able to interfere, and the deed was done.

Hiram's Hospital, as the retreat is called, is a picturesque building enough, and shows the correct taste with which the ecclesiastical architects of those days were imbued. It stands on the banks of the little river, which flows nearly round the cathedral close, being on the side furthest from the town. The London road crosses the river by a pretty one-arched bridge, and, looking from this bridge, the stranger will see the windows of the old men's rooms, each pair of windows separated by a small buttress. A broad gravel walk runs between the building and the river, which is always trim and cared for; and at the end of the walk, under the parapet of the approach to the bridge, is a large and well-worn seat, on which, in mild weather, three or four of Hiram's bedesmen are sure to be seen seated. Beyond this row of buttresses, and further from the bridge, and also further from the water which here suddenly bends, are the pretty oriel windows of Mr Harding's house, and his (Harding) well-mown lawn. The entrance to the hospital is from the London road, and is made through a ponderous gateway under a heavy stone arch, unnecessary, one would suppose, at any time, for the protection of twelve old men, but greatly conducive to the good appearance of Hiram's charity. On passing through this portal, never closed to anyone from 6 A.M. till 10 P.M., and never open afterwards, except on application to a huge, intricately hung medi√¶val bell, the handle of which no uninitiated intruder can possibly find, the six doors of the old men's abodes are seen, and beyond them is a slight iron screen, through which the more happy portion of the Barchester elite pass into the Elysium of Mr Harding's dwelling.

Mr Harding is a small man, now verging on sixty years, but bearing few of the signs of age; his (Harding) hair is rather grizzled, though not gray; his (Harding) eye is very mild, but clear and bright, though the double glasses which are held swinging from his (Harding) hand, unless when fixed upon his (Harding) nose, show that time has told upon his (Harding) sight; his (Harding) hands are delicately white, and both hands and feet are small; he (Harding) always wears a black frock coat, black knee-breeches, and black gaiters, and somewhat scandalises some of his (Harding) more hyperclerical brethren by a black neck-handkerchief.

Mr Harding's warmest admirers cannot say that he (Harding) was ever an industrious man; the circumstances of his (Harding) life have not called on him (Harding) to be so; and yet he (Harding) can hardly be called an idler. Since his (Harding) appointment to his (Harding) precentorship, he (Harding) has published, with all possible additions of vellum, typography, and gilding, a collection of our ancient church music, with some correct dissertations on Purcell, Crotch, and Nares. He (Purcell) has greatly improved the choir of Barchester, which, under his (Purcell) dominion, now rivals that of any cathedral in England. He (Purcell) has taken something more than his (Purcell) fair share in the cathedral services, and has played the violoncello daily to such audiences as he (Purcell) could collect, or, _faute de mieux_, to no audience at all.

We must mention one other peculiarity of Mr Harding. As we have before stated, he (Harding) has an income of eight hundred a year, and has no family but his (Harding) one daughter; and yet he (Harding) is never quite at ease in money matters. The vellum and gilding of

``Harding's Church Music'' (the author publisher)
cost more than any one knows, except the author, the publisher, and the Rev. Theophilus Grantly, who allows none of his (author) father-in-law's extravagances to escape him (author). Then he (author) is generous to his (author) daughter, for whose service he (author) keeps a small carriage and pair of ponies. He (author) is, indeed, generous to all, but especially to the twelve old men who are in a peculiar manner under his (author) care. No doubt with such an income Mr Harding should be above the world, as the saying is; but, at any rate, he (Harding) is not above Archdeacon Theophilus Grantly, for he (Harding) is always more or less in debt to his (Harding) son-in-law, who has, to a certain extent, assumed the arrangement of the precentor's pecuniary affairs.

Chapter II

THE BARCHESTER REFORMER

Mr Harding has been now precentor of Barchester for ten years; and, alas, the murmurs respecting the proceeds of Hiram's estate are again becoming audible. It is not that any one begrudges to Mr Harding the income which he (Harding) enjoys, and the comfortable place which so well becomes him (Harding); but such matters have begun to be talked of in various parts of England. Eager pushing politicians have asserted in the House of Commons, with very telling indignation, that the grasping priests of the Church of England are gorged with the wealth which the charity of former times has left for the solace of the aged, or the education of the young. The well-known case of the Hospital of St Cross has even come before the law courts of the country, and the struggles of Mr Whiston, at Rochester, have met with sympathy and support. Men are beginning to say that these things must be looked into.

Mr Harding, whose conscience in the matter is clear, and who has never felt that he (Harding) had received a pound from Hiram's will to which he (Hiram) was not entitled, has naturally taken the part of the church in talking over these matters with his (Hiram) friend, the bishop, and his (Hiram) son-in-law, the archdeacon. The archdeacon, indeed, Dr Grantly, has been somewhat loud in the matter. He (Hiram) is a personal friend of the dignitaries of the Rochester Chapter, and has written letters in the public press on the subject of that turbulent Dr Whiston, which, his (Whiston) admirers think, must well nigh set the question at rest. It is also known at Oxford that he (Whiston) is the author of the pamphlet signed

``Sacerdos'' (the author)
on the subject of the Earl of Guildford and St Cross, in which it is so clearly argued that the manners of the present times do not admit of a literal adhesion to the very words of the founder's will, but that the interests of the church for which the founder was so deeply concerned are best consulted in enabling its bishops to reward those shining lights whose services have been most signally serviceable to Christianity. In answer to this, it is asserted that Henry de Blois, founder of St Cross, was not greatly interested in the welfare of the reformed church, and that the masters of St Cross, for many years past, cannot be called shining lights in the service of Christianity; it is, however, stoutly maintained, and no doubt felt, by all the archdeacon's friends, that his (Cross) logic is conclusive, and has not, in fact, been answered.

With such a tower of strength to back both his (Cross) arguments and his (Cross) conscience, it may be imagined that Mr Harding has never felt any compunction as to receiving his (Harding) quarterly sum of two hundred pounds. Indeed, the subject has never presented itself to his (Harding) mind in that shape. He (Harding) has talked not unfrequently, and heard very much about the wills of old founders and the incomes arising from their estates, during the last year or two; he (Harding) did even, at one moment, feel a doubt -LRB-since expelled by his (Harding) son-in-law's logic-RRB- as to whether Lord Guildford was clearly entitled to receive so enormous an income as he (Lord) does from the revenues of St Cross; but that he (Cross) himself was overpaid with his (Cross) modest eight hundred pounds,--he (Cross) who, out of that, voluntarily gave up sixty-two pounds eleven shillings and fourpence a year to his (Cross) twelve old neighbours,--he (Cross) who, for the money, does his (Cross) precentor's work as no precentor has done it before, since Barchester Cathedral was built,--such an idea has never sullied his (Cross) quiet, or disturbed his (Cross) conscience.

Nevertheless, Mr Harding is becoming uneasy at the rumour which he (Harding) knows to prevail in Barchester on the subject. He (Harding) is aware that, at any rate, two of his (Harding) old men have been heard to say, that if everyone had his (Harding) own, they might each have their hundred pounds a year, and live like gentlemen, instead of a beggarly one shilling and sixpence a day; and that they had slender cause to be thankful for a miserable dole of twopence, when Mr Harding and Mr Chadwick, between them, ran away with thousands of pounds which good old John Hiram never intended for the like of them. It is the ingratitude of this which stings Mr Harding. One of this discontented pair, Abel Handy, was put into the hospital by himself; he (Handy) had been a stone-mason in Barchester, and had broken his (Handy) thigh by a fall from a scaffolding, while employed about the cathedral; and Mr Harding had given him (Harding) the first vacancy in the hospital after the occurrence, although Dr Grantly had been very anxious to put into it an insufferable clerk of his (Harding) at Plumstead Episcopi, who had lost all his (Harding) teeth, and whom the archdeacon hardly knew how to get rid of by other means. Dr Grantly has not forgotten to remind Mr Harding how well satisfied with his (Harding) one-and-sixpence a day old Joe Mutters would have been, and how injudicious it was on the part of Mr Harding to allow a radical from the town to get into the concern. Probably Dr Grantly forgot, at the moment, that the charity was intended for broken-down journeymen of Barchester.

There is living at Barchester, a young man, a surgeon, named John Bold, and both Mr Harding and Dr Grantly are well aware that to him (Harding) is owing the pestilent rebellious feeling which has shown itself in the hospital; yes, and the renewal, too, of that disagreeable talk about Hiram's estates which is now again prevalent in Barchester. Nevertheless, Mr Harding and Mr Bold are acquainted with each other; we may say, are friends, considering the great disparity in their years. Dr Grantly, however, has a holy horror of the impious demagogue, as on one occasion he (demagogue) called Bold, when speaking of him (demagogue) to the precentor; and being a more prudent far-seeing man than Mr Harding, and possessed of a stronger head, he (Harding) already perceives that this John Bold will work great trouble in Barchester. He (Harding) considers that he (Harding) is to be regarded as an enemy, and thinks that he (Harding) should not be admitted into the camp on anything like friendly terms. As John Bold will occupy much of our attention, we must endeavour to explain who he (Bold) is, and why he (Bold) takes the part of John Hiram's bedesmen.

John Bold is a young surgeon, who passed many of his (Bold) boyish years at Barchester. His (Bold) father was a physician in the city of London, where he (Bold) made a moderate fortune, which he (Bold) invested in houses in that city. The Dragon of Wantly inn and posting-house belonged to him (Bold), also four shops in the High Street, and a moiety of the new row of genteel villas -LRB-so called in the advertisements-RRB-, built outside the town just beyond Hiram's Hospital. To one of these Dr Bold retired to spend the evening of his (Hiram) life, and to die; and here his (Hiram) son John spent his (John) holidays, and afterwards his (John) Christmas vacation when he (John) went from school to study surgery in the London hospitals. Just as John Bold was entitled to write himself surgeon and apothecary, old Dr Bold died, leaving his (Bold) Barchester property to his (Bold) son, and a certain sum in the three per cents. to his (Bold) daughter Mary, who is some four or five years older than her (Mary) brother.

John Bold determined to settle himself at Barchester, and look after his (Bold) own property, as well as the bones and bodies of such of his (Bold) neighbours as would call upon him (Bold) for assistance in their troubles. He (Bold) therefore put up a large brass plate with

``John Bold, Surgeon'' (He)
on it, to the great disgust of the nine practitioners who were already trying to get a living out of the bishop, dean, and canons; and began house-keeping with the aid of his (dean) sister. At this time he (dean) was not more than twenty-four years old; and though he (dean) has now been three years in Barchester, we have not heard that he (dean) has done much harm to the nine worthy practitioners. Indeed, their dread of him (dean) has died away; for in three years he (dean) has not taken three fees.

Nevertheless, John Bold is a clever man, and would, with practice, be a clever surgeon; but he (surgeon) has got quite into another line of life. Having enough to live on, he (surgeon) has not been forced to work for bread; he (surgeon) has declined to subject himself to what he (surgeon) calls the drudgery of the profession, by which, I believe, he (surgeon) means the general work of a practising surgeon; and has found other employment. He (surgeon) frequently binds up the bruises and sets the limbs of such of the poorer classes as profess his (surgeon) way of thinking,--but this he (surgeon) does for love. Now I will not say that the archdeacon is strictly correct in stigmatising John Bold as a demagogue, for I hardly know how extreme must be a man's opinions before he (man) can be justly so called; but Bold is a strong reformer. His (man) passion is the reform of all abuses; state abuses, church abuses, corporation abuses -LRB-he (man) has got himself elected a town councillor of Barchester, and has so worried three consecutive mayors, that it became somewhat difficult to find a fourth-RRB-, abuses in medical practice, and general abuses in the world at large. Bold is thoroughly sincere in his (man) patriotic endeavours to mend mankind, and there is something to be admired in the energy with which he (man) devotes himself to remedying evil and stopping injustice; but I fear that he (man) is too much imbued with the idea that he (man) has a special mission for reforming. It would be well if one so young had a little more diffidence himself, and more trust in the honest purposes of others,--if he (man) could be brought to believe that old customs need not necessarily be evil, and that changes may possibly be dangerous; but no, Bold has all the ardour and all the self-assurance of a Danton, and hurls his (man) anathemas against time-honoured practices with the violence of a French Jacobin.

No wonder that Dr Grantly should regard Bold as a firebrand, falling, as he (firebrand) has done, almost in the centre of the quiet ancient close of Barchester Cathedral. Dr Grantly would have him (firebrand) avoided as the plague; but the old Doctor and Mr Harding were fast friends. Young Johnny Bold used to play as a boy on Mr Harding's lawn; he (Harding) has many a time won the precentor's heart by listening with rapt attention to his (Harding) sacred strains; and since those days, to tell the truth at once, he (Harding) has nearly won another heart within the same walls.

Eleanor Harding has not plighted her (Harding) troth to John Bold, nor has she (Harding), perhaps, owned to herself how dear to her (Harding) the young reformer is; but she (Harding) cannot endure that anyone should speak harshly of him (reformer). She (Harding) does not dare to defend him (reformer) when her (Harding) brother-in-law is so loud against him (brother-in-law); for she (Harding), like her (Harding) father, is somewhat afraid of Dr Grantly; but she (Harding) is beginning greatly to dislike the archdeacon. She (Harding) persuades her (Harding) father that it would be both unjust and injudicious to banish his (father) young friend because of his (father) politics; she (Harding) cares little to go to houses where she (Harding) will not meet him (father), and, in fact, she (Harding) is in love.

Nor is there any good reason why Eleanor Harding should not love John Bold. He (Bold) has all those qualities which are likely to touch a girl's heart. He (Bold) is brave, eager, and amusing; well-made and good-looking; young and enterprising; his (Bold) character is in all respects good; he (Bold) has sufficient income to support a wife; he (Bold) is her (wife) father's friend; and, above all, he (Bold) is in love with her (wife): then why should not Eleanor Harding be attached to John Bold?

Dr Grantly, who has as many eyes as Argus, and has long seen how the wind blows in that direction, thinks there are various strong reasons why this should not be so. He (Bold) has not thought it wise as yet to speak to his (Bold) father-in-law on the subject, for he (Bold) knows how foolishly indulgent is Mr Harding in everything that concerns his (Harding) daughter; but he (Harding) has discussed the matter with his (Harding) all-trusted helpmate, within that sacred recess formed by the clerical bed-curtains at Plumstead Episcopi.

How much sweet solace, how much valued counsel has our archdeacon received within that sainted enclosure! ``Tis there alone that he (Bold) unbends, and comes down from his (Bold) high church pedestal to the level of a mortal man. In the world Dr Grantly never lays aside that demeanour which so well becomes him (Bold). He (Bold) has all the dignity of an ancient saint with the sleekness of a modern bishop; he (bishop) is always the same; he (bishop) is always the archdeacon; unlike Homer, he (Homer) never nods. Even with his (Homer) father-in-law, even with the bishop and dean, he (Homer) maintains that sonorous tone and lofty deportment which strikes awe into the young hearts of Barchester, and absolutely cows the whole parish of Plumstead Episcopi. ``Tis only when he (Homer) has exchanged that ever-new shovel hat for a tasselled nightcap, and those shining black habiliments for his (Homer) accustomed _robe de nuit_, that Dr Grantly talks, and looks, and thinks like an ordinary man.

Many of us have often thought how severe a trial of faith must this be to the wives of our great church dignitaries. To us these men are personifications of St Paul; their very gait is a speaking sermon; their clean and sombre apparel exacts from us faith and submission, and the cardinal virtues seem to hover round their sacred hats. A dean or archbishop, in the garb of his (dean) order, is sure of our reverence, and a well-got-up bishop fills our very souls with awe. Chapter III

THE BISHOP OF BARCHESTER

Bold at once repaired to the hospital. It was amusing to see the positions, and eager listening faces of these well-to-do old men. The bishop and Mr Harding loved each other warmly. They had grown old together, and had together spent many, many years in clerical pursuits and clerical conversation. I will not say that they managed the diocese between them, but they spent much time in discussing the man who did, and in forming little plans to mitigate his (man) wrath against church delinquents, and soften his (man) aspirations for church dominion.

Chapter IV

HIRAM``S BEDESMEN

The parties most interested in the movement which is about to set Barchester by the ears were not the foremost to discuss the merit of the question, as is often the case; but when the bishop, the archdeacon, the warden, the steward, and Messrs Cox and Cummins, were all busy with the matter, each in his (Cummins) own way, it is not to be supposed that Hiram's bedesmen themselves were altogether passive spectators. Finney, the attorney, had been among them, asking sly questions, and raising immoderate hopes, creating a party hostile to the warden, and establishing a corps in the enemy's camp, as he (warden) figuratively calls it to himself. Poor old men: whoever may be righted or wronged by this inquiry, they at any rate will assuredly be only injured: to them it can only be an unmixed evil. How can their lot be improved? all their wants are supplied; every comfort is administered; they have warm houses, good clothes, plentiful diet, and rest after a life of labour; and above all, that treasure so inestimable in declining years, a true and kind friend to listen to their sorrows, watch over their sickness, and administer comfort as regards this world, and the world to come!

John Bold sometimes thinks of this, when he (Bold) is talking loudly of the rights of the bedesmen, whom he (Bold) has taken under his (Bold) protection; but he (Bold) quiets the suggestion within his (Bold) breast with the high-sounding name of justice:

''_Fiat justitia, ruat coelum_.'' (he)
These old men should, by rights, have one hundred pounds a year instead of one shilling and sixpence a day, and the warden should have two hundred or three hundred pounds instead of eight hundred pounds. What is unjust must be wrong; what is wrong should be righted; and if he (warden) declined the task, who else would do it?

``Each one of you is clearly entitled to one hundred pounds a year by common law'' (Finney/Mr Finney)
: such had been the important whisper made by Finney into the ears of Abel Handy, and by him (Handy) retailed to his (Handy) eleven brethren.

The great Bunce was not to be wiled away, and was upheld in his (Bunce) orthodoxy by two adherents. Abel Handy, who was the leader of the aspirants after wealth, had, alas, a stronger following. The other three, volatile unstable minds, vacillated between the two chieftains, now led away by the hope of gold, now anxious to propitiate the powers that still existed.

It had been proposed to address a petition to the bishop as visitor, praying his (visitor) lordship to see justice done to the legal recipients of John Hiram's Charity, and to send copies of this petition and of the reply it would elicit to all the leading London papers, and thereby to obtain notoriety for the subject. This it was thought would pave the way for ulterior legal proceedings. It would have been a great thing to have had the signatures and marks of all the twelve injured legatees; but this was impossible: Bunce would have cut his (Bunce) hand off sooner than have signed it. It was then suggested by Finney that if even eleven could be induced to sanction the document, the one obstinate recusant might have been represented as unfit to judge on such a question,--in fact, as being _non compos mentis_,--and the petition would have been taken as representing the feeling of the men. But this could not be done: Bunce's friends were as firm as himself, and as yet only six crosses adorned the document. ``Job Skulpit;'' (he)

Chapter V

DR GRANTLY VISITS THE HOSPITAL

Though doubt and hesitation disturbed the rest of our poor warden, no such weakness perplexed the nobler breast of his (warden) son-in-law. As the indomitable cock preparing for the combat sharpens his (warden) spurs, shakes his (warden) feathers, and erects his (warden) comb, so did the archdeacon arrange his (warden) weapons for the coming war, without misgiving and without fear. That he (warden) was fully confident of the justice of his (warden) cause let no one doubt. Many a man can fight his (man) battle with good courage, but with a doubting conscience. Such was not the case with Dr Grantly. He (man) did not believe in the Gospel with more assurance than he (man) did in the sacred justice of all ecclesiastical revenues. When he (man) put his (man) shoulder to the wheel to defend the income of the present and future precentors of Barchester, he (man) was animated by as strong a sense of a holy cause, as that which gives courage to a missionary in Africa, or enables a sister of mercy to give up the pleasures of the world for the wards of a hospital. He (man) was about to defend the holy of holies from the touch of the profane; to guard the citadel of his (man) church from the most rampant of its enemies; to put on his (man) good armour in the best of fights, and secure, if possible, the comforts of his (man) creed for coming generations of ecclesiastical dignitaries. Such a work required no ordinary vigour; and the archdeacon was, therefore, extraordinarily vigorous. It demanded a buoyant courage, and a heart happy in its toil; and the archdeacon's heart was happy, and his (man) courage was buoyant.

He (man) knew that he (man) would not be able to animate his (man) father-in-law with feelings like his (man) own, but this did not much disturb him (man). He (man) preferred to bear the brunt of the battle alone, and did not doubt that the warden would resign himself into his (warden) hands with passive submission.

``Well, Mr Chadwick,'' (he)
he (warden) said, walking into the steward's office a day or two after the signing of the petition as commemorated in the last chapter:
``anything from Cox and Cummins this morning?'' (the steward)

``I quite agree with them,'' (Dr Grantly)
said Dr Grantly, refolding the letter.
``I perfectly agree with them. Haphazard is no doubt the best man; a thorough churchman, a sound conservative, and in every respect the best man we could get;--he (man)'s in the House, too, which is a great thing.'' (Mr Chadwick/Chadwick)

Mr Chadwick quite agreed.

``You remember how completely he (man) put down that scoundrel Horseman about the Bishop of Beverley's income; how completely he (Bishop) set them all adrift in the earl's case.'' (St Cross)
Since the question of St Cross had been mooted by the public, one noble lord had become
``the earl,'' (lord)

With all his (doctor) admiration for Sir Abraham, the doctor seemed to think it not impossible that that great man might be induced to lend his (man) gigantic powers to the side of the church's enemies.

Having settled this point to his (man) satisfaction, the doctor stepped down to the hospital, to learn how matters were going on there; and as he (man) walked across the hallowed close, and looked up at the ravens who cawed with a peculiar reverence as he (man) wended his (man) way, he (man) thought with increased acerbity of those whose impiety would venture to disturb the goodly grace of cathedral institutions.

And who has not felt the same? We believe that Mr Horseman himself would relent, and the spirit of Sir Benjamin Hall give way, were those great reformers to allow themselves to stroll by moonlight round the towers of some of our ancient churches. Who would not feel charity for a prebendary when walking the quiet length of that long aisle at Winchester, looking at those decent houses, that trim grass-plat, and feeling, as one must, the solemn, orderly comfort of the spot! Who could be hard upon a dean while wandering round the sweet close of Hereford, and owning that in that precinct, tone and colour, design and form, solemn tower and storied window, are all in unison, and all perfect! Who could lie basking in the cloisters of Salisbury, and gaze on Jewel's library and that unequalled spire, without feeling that bishops should sometimes be rich!

The tone of our archdeacon's mind must not astonish us; it has been the growth of centuries of church ascendancy; and though some fungi now disfigure the tree, though there be much dead wood, for how much good fruit have not we to be thankful? Who, without remorse, can batter down the dead branches of an old oak, now useless, but, ah! still so beautiful, or drag out the fragments of the ancient forest, without feeling that they sheltered the younger plants, to which they are now summoned to give way in a tone so peremptory and so harsh?

The archdeacon, with all his (we) virtues, was not a man of delicate feeling; and after having made his (we) morning salutations in the warden's drawing-room, he (we) did not scruple to commence an attack on

``pestilent'' (he)

The archdeacon, who was a practical man, allowed himself the use of everyday expressive modes of speech when among his (man) closest intimates, though no one could soar into a more intricate labyrinth of refined phraseology when the church was the subject, and his (man) lower brethren were his (man) auditors.

Mr Harding knew not how to resist, and the disagreeable order was given. The quad, as it was familiarly called, was a small quadrangle, open on one side to the river, and surrounded on the others by the high wall of Mr Harding's garden, by one gable end of Mr Harding's house, and by the end of the row of buildings which formed the residences of the bedesmen. It was flagged all round, and the centre was stoned; small stone gutters ran from the four corners of the square to a grating in the centre; and attached to the end of Mr Harding's house was a conduit with four cocks covered over from the weather, at which the old men got their water, and very generally performed their morning toilet. It was a quiet, sombre place, shaded over by the trees of the warden's garden. On the side towards the river, there stood a row of stone seats, on which the old men would sit and gaze at the little fish, as they flitted by in the running stream. On the other side of the river was a rich, green meadow, running up to and joining the deanery, and as little open to the public as the garden of the dean itself. Nothing, therefore, could be more private than the quad of the hospital; and it was there that the archdeacon determined to convey to them his (dean) sense of their refractory proceedings.

The servant soon brought in word that the men were assembled in the quad, and the archdeacon, big with his (dean) purpose, rose to address them.

``Well, warden, of course you're coming,'' (he)
said he (dean), seeing that Mr Harding did not prepare to follow him (Harding).

``I wish you'd excuse me,'' (Mr Harding/Harding)

The old men were assembled in groups in the quadrangle--eleven of them at least, for poor old Johnny Bell was bed-ridden, and couldn't come; he (Bell) had, however, put his (Bell) mark to the petition, as one of Handy's earliest followers. ``Tis true he (warden) could not move from the bed where he (warden) lay; `tis true he (warden) had no friend on earth, but those whom the hospital contained; and of those the warden and his (warden) daughter were the most constant and most appreciated; `tis true that everything was administered to him (warden) which his (warden) failing body could require, or which his (warden) faint appetite could enjoy; but still his (warden) dull eye had glistened for a moment at the idea of possessing a hundred pounds a year

``to his (warden) own cheek,'' (unknown)

``and the doctor's lady,'' (Miss Eleanor)

As the archdeacon stood up to make his (warden) speech, erect in the middle of that little square, he (warden) looked like an ecclesiastical statue placed there, as a fitting impersonation of the church militant here on earth; his (warden) shovel hat, large, new, and well-pronounced, a churchman's hat in every inch, declared the profession as plainly as does the Quaker's broad brim; his (warden) heavy eyebrows, large open eyes, and full mouth and chin expressed the solidity of his (warden) order; the broad chest, amply covered with fine cloth, told how well to do was its estate; one hand ensconced within his (warden) pocket, evinced the practical hold which our mother church keeps on her (mother) temporal possessions; and the other, loose for action, was ready to fight if need be in her (mother) defence; and, below these, the decorous breeches, and neat black gaiters showing so admirably that well-turned leg, betokened the decency, the outward beauty and grace of our church establishment.

``Now, my men,'' (he)
he (warden) began, when he (warden) had settled himself well in his (warden) position,
``I want to say a few words to you. Your good friend, the warden here, and myself, and my lord the bishop, on whose behalf I wish to speak to you, would all be very sorry, very sorry indeed, that you should have any just ground of complaint. Any just ground of complaint on your part would be removed at once by the warden, or by his (warden) lordship, or by me on his (warden) behalf, without the necessity of any petition on your part.'' (he)
Here the orator stopped for a moment, expecting that some little murmurs of applause would show that the weakest of the men were beginning to give way; but no such murmurs came. Bunce, himself, even sat with closed lips, mute and unsatisfactory.
``Without the necessity of any petition at all,'' (he)
he (Bunce) repeated.

He (Bunce) paused for a reply from the men, and after a while, Handy plucked up courage and said,

Here most of the men expressed their assent.
``Now what is it you ask for? What is it you want that you hav'n't got here? What is it--'' (Moody)

``A hundred a year,'' (Moody)

``A hundred a year!'' (the archdeacon)
ejaculated the archdeacon militant, defying the impudence of these claimants with one hand stretched out and closed, while with the other he (Moody) tightly grasped, and secured within his (Moody) breeches pocket, that symbol of the church's wealth which his (Moody) own loose half-crowns not unaptly represented.
``A hundred a year! Why, my men, you must be mad; and you talk about John Hiram's will! When John Hiram built a hospital for worn-out old men, worn-out old labouring men, infirm old men past their work, cripples, blind, bed-ridden, and such like, do you think he (Hiram) meant to make gentlemen of them? Do you think John Hiram intended to give a hundred a year to old single men, who earned perhaps two shillings or half-a-crown a day for themselves and families in the best of their time? No, my men, I'll tell you what John Hiram meant: he (Hiram) meant that twelve poor old worn-out labourers, men who could no longer support themselves, who had no friends to support them, who must starve and perish miserably if not protected by the hand of charity;--he (Hiram) meant that twelve such men as these should come in here in their poverty and wretchedness, and find within these walls shelter and food before their death, and a little leisure to make their peace with God. That was what John Hiram meant: you have not read John Hiram's will, and I doubt whether those wicked men who are advising you have done so. I have; I know what his (Hiram) will was; and I tell you that that was his (Hiram) will, and that that was his (Hiram) intention.'' (the archdeacon)

Not a sound came from the eleven bedesmen, as they sat listening to what, according to the archdeacon, was their intended estate. They grimly stared upon his (Moody) burly figure, but did not then express, by word or sign, the anger and disgust to which such language was sure to give rise.

``Now let me ask you,'' (he)
he (Moody) continued:
``do you think you are worse off than John Hiram intended to make you? Have you not shelter, and food, and leisure? Have you not much more? Have you not every indulgence which you are capable of enjoying? Have you not twice better food, twice a better bed, ten times more money in your pocket than you were ever able to earn for yourselves before you were lucky enough to get into this place? And now you send a petition to the bishop, asking for a hundred pounds a year! I tell you what, my friends; you are deluded, and made fools of by wicked men who are acting for their own ends. You will never get a hundred pence a year more than what you have now: it is very possible that you may get less; it is very possible that my lord the bishop, and your warden, may make changes--'' (he)

``No, no, no,'' (Mr Harding/Harding)
interrupted Mr Harding, who had been listening with indescribable misery to the tirade of his (Moody) son-in-law;
``no, my friends. I want no changes,--at least no changes that shall make you worse off than you now are, as long as you and I live together.'' (son-in-law)

The archdeacon had been interrupted in his (Moody) speech before he (Moody) had quite finished it; but he (Moody) felt that he (Moody) could not recommence with dignity after this little ebullition, and he (Moody) led the way back into the garden, followed by his (Moody) father-in-law.

``Well,'' (he)

The warden agreed. He (warden) certainly thought they had understood to the full what had been said to them.

``They know pretty well what they have to expect from us; they know how we shall meet any refractory spirit on their part; they know that we are not afraid of them. The warden's mind was very full,--full nearly to overcharging itself; and had it done so,--had he (warden) allowed himself to speak the thoughts which were working within him (warden), he (warden) would indeed have astonished the archdeacon by the reprobation he (warden) would have expressed as to the proceeding of which he (warden) had been so unwilling a witness. But different feelings kept him (warden) silent; he (warden) was as yet afraid of differing from his (warden) son-in-law;--he (warden) was anxious beyond measure to avoid even a semblance of rupture with any of his (warden) order, and was painfully fearful of having to come to an open quarrel with any person on any subject. His (warden) life had hitherto been so quiet, so free from strife; his (warden) little early troubles had required nothing but passive fortitude; his (warden) subsequent prosperity had never forced upon him (warden) any active cares,--had never brought him (warden) into disagreeable contact with anyone. He (warden) felt that he (warden) would give almost anything,--much more than he (warden) knew he (warden) ought to do,--to relieve himself from the storm which he (warden) feared was coming. It was so hard that the pleasant waters of his (warden) little stream should be disturbed and muddied by rough hands; that his (warden) quiet paths should be made a battlefield; that the unobtrusive corner of the world which had been allotted to him (warden), as though by Providence, should be invaded and desecrated, and all within it made miserable and unsound.

Money he (warden) had none to give; the knack of putting guineas together had never belonged to him (warden); but how willingly, with what a foolish easiness, with what happy alacrity, would he (warden) have abandoned the half of his (warden) income for all time to come, could he (warden) by so doing have quietly dispelled the clouds that were gathering over him (warden),--could he (warden) have thus compromised the matter between the reformer and the conservative, between his (warden) possible son-in-law, Bold, and his (warden) positive son-in-law, the archdeacon.

And this compromise would not have been made from any prudential motive of saving what would yet remain, for Mr Harding still felt little doubt but he (Harding) should be left for life in quiet possession of the good things he (Harding) had, if he (Harding) chose to retain them. No; he (Harding) would have done so from the sheer love of quiet, and from a horror of being made the subject of public talk. He (Harding) had very often been moved to pity.--to that inward weeping of the heart for others' woes; but none had he (Harding) ever pitied more than that old lord, whose almost fabulous wealth, drawn from his (Harding) church preferments, had become the subject of so much opprobrium, of such public scorn; that wretched clerical octogenarian Croesus, whom men would not allow to die in peace,--whom all the world united to decry and to abhor.

Was he (octogenarian) to suffer such a fate? Was his (octogenarian) humble name to be bandied in men's mouths, as the gormandiser of the resources of the poor, as of one who had filched from the charity of other ages wealth which had been intended to relieve the old and the infirm? Was he (octogenarian) to be gibbeted in the press, to become a byword for oppression, to be named as an example of the greed of the English church? Should it ever be said that he (octogenarian) had robbed those old men, whom he (octogenarian) so truly and so tenderly loved in his (octogenarian) heart of hearts? As he (octogenarian) slowly paced, hour after hour, under those noble lime-trees, turning these sad thoughts within him (octogenarian), he (octogenarian) became all but fixed in his (octogenarian) resolve that some great step must be taken to relieve him (octogenarian) from the risk of so terrible a fate.

In the meanwhile, the archdeacon, with contented mind and unruffled spirit, went about his (octogenarian) business. He (octogenarian) said a word or two to Mr Chadwick, and then finding, as he (Chadwick) expected, the petition lying in his (Chadwick) father's library, he (Chadwick) wrote a short answer to the men, in which he (Chadwick) told them that they had no evils to redress, but rather great mercies for which to be thankful; and having seen the bishop sign it, he (bishop) got into his (bishop) brougham and returned home to Mrs Grantly, and Plumstead Episcopi.

Chapter VI

THE WARDEN``S TEA PARTY

After much painful doubting, on one thing only could Mr Harding resolve. He (Harding) determined that at any rate he (Harding) would take no offence, and that he (Harding) would make this question no cause of quarrel either with Bold or with the bedesmen. In furtherance of this resolution, he (Harding) himself wrote a note to Mr Bold, the same afternoon, inviting him (Mr) to meet a few friends and hear some music on an evening named in the next week. Had not this little party been promised to Eleanor, in his (Mr) present state of mind he (Mr) would probably have avoided such gaiety; but the promise had been given, the invitations were to be written, and when Eleanor consulted her (Eleanor) father on the subject, she (Eleanor) was not ill pleased to hear him (father) say,

``Oh, I was thinking of Bold, so I took it into my head to write to him (father) myself, but you must write to his (father) sister.'' (him)

Mary Bold was older than her (Bold) brother, and, at the time of our story, was just over thirty. She (Bold) was not an unattractive young woman, though by no means beautiful. Her (Bold) great merit was the kindliness of her (Bold) disposition. She (Bold) was not very clever, nor very animated, nor had she (Bold) apparently the energy of her (Bold) brother; but she (Bold) was guided by a high principle of right and wrong; her (Bold) temper was sweet, and her (Bold) faults were fewer in number than her (Bold) virtues. Those who casually met Mary Bold thought little of her (Bold); but those who knew her (Bold) well loved her (Bold) well, and the longer they knew her (Bold) the more they loved her (Bold). Among those who were fondest of her (Bold) was Eleanor Harding; and though Eleanor had never openly talked to her (Eleanor) of her (Eleanor) brother, each understood the other's feelings about him (brother). The brother and sister were sitting together when the two notes were brought in.

``How odd,'' (Mary/Mary Bold)
said Mary,
``that they should send two notes. Well, if Mr Harding becomes fashionable, the world is going to change.'' (Mary/Mary Bold)

Her (Mary) brother understood immediately the nature and intention of the peace-offering; but it was not so easy for him (brother) to behave well in the matter, as it was for Mr Harding. It is much less difficult for the sufferer to be generous than for the oppressor. John Bold felt that he (Bold) could not go to the warden's party: he (Bold) never loved Eleanor better than he (Bold) did now; he (Bold) had never so strongly felt how anxious he (Bold) was to make her (Eleanor) his (Bold) wife as now, when so many obstacles to his (Bold) doing so appeared in view. Yet here was her (Eleanor) father himself, as it were, clearing away those very obstacles, and still he (Bold) felt that he (Bold) could not go to the house any more as an open friend.

As he (Bold) sat thinking of these things with the note in his (Bold) hand, his (Bold) sister was waiting for his (Bold) decision.

``Well,'' (she)
said she (sister),
``I suppose we must write separate answers, and both say we shall be very happy.'' (she)

``You'll go, of course, Mary,'' (he)
said he (Bold); to which she (sister) readily assented.
``I cannot,'' (he)
he (Bold) continued, looking serious and gloomy.
``I wish I could, with all my heart.'' (unknown)

``And why not, John?'' (she)
said she (sister). She (sister) had as yet heard nothing of the new-found abuse which her (sister) brother was about to reform;--at least nothing which connected it with her (sister) brother's name.

He (brother) sat thinking for a while till he (brother) determined that it would be best to tell her (sister) at once what it was that he (brother) was about: it must be done sooner or later.

``I fear I cannot go to Mr Harding's house any more as a friend, just at present.'' (unknown)

``No, indeed,'' (he)
said he (brother);
``I've no quarrel with her (Eleanor) as yet.'' (he)

``Why,'' (he)

``I've taken up the case of these twelve old men of Hiram's Hospital, and of course that brings me into contact with Mr Harding. I may have to oppose him (Harding), interfere with him (Harding),--perhaps injure him (Harding).'' (he)

Mary looked at him (brother) steadily for some time before she (Mary) committed herself to reply, and then merely asked him (brother) what he (brother) meant to do for the old men.

``Why, it's a long story, and I don't know that I can make you understand it. John Hiram made a will, and left his (Hiram) property in charity for certain poor old men, and the proceeds, instead of going to the benefit of these men, go chiefly into the pocket of the warden and the bishop's steward.'' (unknown)

The party went off as such parties do. There were fat old ladies, in fine silk dresses, and slim young ladies, in gauzy muslin frocks; old gentlemen stood up with their backs to the empty fire-place, looking by no means so comfortable as they would have done in their own arm-chairs at home; and young gentlemen, rather stiff about the neck, clustered near the door, not as yet sufficiently in courage to attack the muslin frocks, who awaited the battle, drawn up in a semicircular array. Loud above all voices was heard the clear sonorous tones of the archdeacon as he (warden) dilated to brother parsons of the danger of the church, of the fearful rumours of mad reforms even at Oxford, and of the damnable heresies of Dr Whiston.

Soon, however, sweeter sounds began timidly to make themselves audible. Little movements were made in a quarter notable for round stools and music stands. Wax candles were arranged in sconces, big books were brought from hidden recesses, and the work of the evening commenced.

How often were those pegs twisted and re-twisted before our friend found that he (friend) had twisted them enough; how many discordant scrapes gave promise of the coming harmony. And now the crash begins: away they go in full flow of harmony together,--up hill and down dale,--now louder and louder, then lower and lower; now loud, as though stirring the battle; then low, as though mourning the slain. In all, through all, and above all, is heard the violoncello. Ah, not for nothing were those pegs so twisted and re-twisted;--listen, listen! Now alone that saddest of instruments tells its touching tale. Silent, and in awe, stand fiddle, flute, and piano, to hear the sorrows of their wailing brother. ``Tis but for a moment: before the melancholy of those low notes has been fully realised, again comes the full force of all the band;--down go the pedals, away rush twenty fingers scouring over the bass notes with all the impetus of passion. Apollo blows till his (father) stiff neckcloth is no better than a rope, and the minor canon works with both arms till he (father) falls in a syncope of exhaustion against the wall.

How comes it that now, when all should be silent, when courtesy, if not taste, should make men listen,--how is it at this moment the black-coated corps leave their retreat and begin skirmishing? One by one they creep forth, and fire off little guns timidly, and without precision. Ah, my men, efforts such as these will take no cities, even though the enemy should be never so open to assault. At length a more deadly artillery is brought to bear; slowly, but with effect, the advance is made; the muslin ranks are broken, and fall into confusion; the formidable array of chairs gives way; the battle is no longer between opposing regiments, but hand to hand, and foot to foot with single combatants, as in the glorious days of old, when fighting was really noble. In corners, and under the shadow of curtains, behind sofas and half hidden by doors, in retiring windows, and sheltered by hanging tapestry, are blows given and returned, fatal, incurable, dealing death.

Apart from this another combat arises, more sober and more serious. The archdeacon is engaged against two prebendaries, a pursy full-blown rector assisting him (rector), in all the perils and all the enjoyments of short whist. With solemn energy do they watch the shuffled pack, and, all-expectant, eye the coming trump. With what anxious nicety do they arrange their cards, jealous of each other's eyes! Why is that lean doctor so slow,--cadaverous man with hollow jaw and sunken eye, ill beseeming the richness of his (doctor) mother church! Ah, why so slow, thou meagre doctor? Vain is the hope, if hope there be, to disturb that meagre doctor. Chapter VII

_THE JUPITER_

Though Eleanor Harding rode off from John Bold on a high horse, it must not be supposed that her (Harding) heart was so elate as her (Harding) demeanour. In the first place, she (Harding) had a natural repugnance to losing her (Harding) lover; and in the next, she (Harding) was not quite so sure that she (Harding) was in the right as she (Harding) pretended to be. Her (Harding) father had told her (Harding), and that now repeatedly, that Bold was doing nothing unjust or ungenerous; and why then should she (Harding) rebuke him (father), and throw him (father) off, when she (Harding) felt herself so ill able to bear his (father) loss?--but such is human nature, and young-lady-nature especially.

As she (Harding) walked off from him (father) beneath the shady elms of the close, her (Harding) look, her (Harding) tone, every motion and gesture of her (Harding) body, belied her (Harding) heart; she (Harding) would have given the world to have taken him (father) by the hand, to have reasoned with him (father), persuaded him (father), cajoled him (father), coaxed him (father) out of his (father) project; to have overcome him (father) with all her (Harding) female artillery, and to have redeemed her (Harding) father at the cost of herself; but pride would not let her (Harding) do this, and she (Harding) left him (father) without a look of love or a word of kindness.

Had Bold been judging of another lover and of another lady, he (lover) might have understood all this as well as we do; but in matters of love men do not see clearly in their own affairs. They say that faint heart never won fair lady; and it is amazing to me how fair ladies are won, so faint are often men's hearts! Were it not for the kindness of their nature, that seeing the weakness of our courage they will occasionally descend from their impregnable fortresses, and themselves aid us in effecting their own defeat, too often would they escape unconquered if not unscathed, and free of body if not of heart.

Poor Bold crept off quite crestfallen; he (us) felt that as regarded Eleanor Harding his (us) fate was sealed, unless he (us) could consent to give up a task to which he (us) had pledged himself, and which indeed it would not be easy for him (us) to give up. Lawyers were engaged, and the question had to a certain extent been taken up by the public; besides, how could a high-spirited girl like Eleanor Harding really learn to love a man for neglecting a duty which he (man) assumed! Could she (Harding) allow her (Harding) affection to be purchased at the cost of his (man) own self-respect?

As regarded the issue of his (man) attempt at reformation in the hospital, Bold had no reason hitherto to be discontented with his (man) success. All Barchester was by the ears about it. The bishop, the archdeacon, the warden, the steward, and several other clerical allies, had daily meetings, discussing their tactics, and preparing for the great attack. Sir Abraham Haphazard had been consulted, but his (Haphazard) opinion was not yet received: copies of Hiram's will, copies of wardens' journals, copies of leases, copies of accounts, copies of everything that could be copied, and of some that could not, had been sent to him (Haphazard); and the case was assuming most creditable dimensions. But, above all, it had been mentioned in the daily _Jupiter_. That all-powerful organ of the press in one of its leading thunderbolts launched at St Cross, had thus remarked:

``Another case, of smaller dimensions indeed, but of similar import, is now likely to come under public notice. We are informed that the warden or master of an old almshouse attached to Barchester Cathedral is in receipt of twenty-five times the annual income appointed for him (warden) by the will of the founder, while the sum yearly expended on the absolute purposes of the charity has always remained fixed. In other words, the legatees under the founder's will have received no advantage from the increase in the value of the property during the last four centuries, such increase having been absorbed by the so-called warden. It is impossible to conceive a case of greater injustice. It is no answer to say that some six or nine or twelve old men receive as much of the goods of this world as such old men require. On what foundation, moral or divine, traditional or legal, is grounded the warden's claim to the large income he (warden) receives for doing nothing? The contentment of these almsmen, if content they be, can give him (warden) no title to this wealth! Does he (warden) ever ask himself, when he (warden) stretches wide his (warden) clerical palm to receive the pay of some dozen of the working clergy, for what service he (warden) is so remunerated? Does his (warden) conscience ever entertain the question of his (warden) right to such subsidies? Or is it possible that the subject never so presents itself to his (warden) mind; that he (warden) has received for many years, and intends, should God spare him (God), to receive for years to come these fruits of the industrious piety of past ages, indifferent as to any right on his (God) own part, or of any injustice to others! We must express an opinion that nowhere but in the Church of England, and only there among its priests, could such a state of moral indifference be found.'' (St Cross)

I must for the present leave my readers to imagine the state of Mr Harding's mind after reading the above article. They say that forty thousand copies of _The Jupiter_ are daily sold, and that each copy is read by five persons at the least. Two hundred thousand readers then would hear this accusation against him (Harding); two hundred thousand hearts would swell with indignation at the griping injustice, the barefaced robbery of the warden of Barchester Hospital! And how was he (warden) to answer this? How was he (warden) to open his (warden) inmost heart to this multitude, to these thousands, the educated, the polished, the picked men of his (warden) own country; how show them that he (warden) was no robber, no avaricious, lazy priest scrambling for gold, but a retiring, humble-spirited man, who had innocently taken what had innocently been offered to him (man)?

``Write to _The Jupiter_,'' (the bishop)
suggested the bishop.

``Yes,'' (the archdeacon)
said the archdeacon, more worldly wise than his (man) father,
``yes, and be smothered with ridicule; tossed over and over again with scorn; shaken this way and that, as a rat in the mouth of a practised terrier. You will leave out some word or letter in your answer, and the ignorance of the cathedral clergy will be harped upon; you will make some small mistake, which will be a falsehood, or some admission, which will be self-condemnation; you will find yourself to have been vulgar, ill-tempered, irreverend, and illiterate, and the chances are ten to one, but that being a clergyman, you will have been guilty of blasphemy! A man may have the best of causes, the best of talents, and the best of tempers; he (man) may write as well as Addison, or as strongly as Junius; but even with all this he (Addison) cannot successfully answer, when attacked by _The Jupiter_. In such matters it is omnipotent. What the Czar is in Russia, or the mob in America, that _The Jupiter_ is in England. Answer such an article! No, warden; whatever you do, don't do that. We were to look for this sort of thing, you know; but we need not draw down on our heads more of it than is necessary.'' (father)

The article in _The Jupiter_, while it so greatly harassed our poor warden, was an immense triumph to some of the opposite party. Sorry as Bold was to see Mr Harding attacked so personally, it still gave him (Harding) a feeling of elation to find his (Harding) cause taken up by so powerful an advocate: and as to Finney, the attorney, he (Finney) was beside himself. What! to be engaged in the same cause and on the same side with _The Jupiter_; to have the views he (Finney) had recommended seconded, and furthered, and battled for by _The Jupiter_! Perhaps to have his (Finney) own name mentioned as that of the learned gentleman whose efforts had been so successful on behalf of the poor of Barchester! He (Finney) might be examined before committees of the House of Commons, with heaven knows how much a day for his (Finney) personal expenses;--he (Finney) might be engaged for years on such a suit! There was no end to the glorious golden dreams which this leader in _The Jupiter_ produced in the soaring mind of Finney.

And the old bedesmen, they also heard of this article, and had a glimmering, indistinct idea of the marvellous advocate which had now taken up their cause. Abel Handy limped hither and thither through the rooms, repeating all that he (Handy) understood to have been printed, with some additions of his (Handy) own which he (Handy) thought should have been added. He (Handy) told them how _The Jupiter_ had declared that their warden was no better than a robber, and that what _The Jupiter_ said was acknowledged by the world to be true. How _The Jupiter_ had affirmed that each one of them--

``each one of us, Jonathan Crumple, think of that!'' (unknown)
--had a clear right to a hundred a year; and that if _The Jupiter_ had said so, it was better than a decision of the Lord Chancellor: and then he (Chancellor) carried about the paper, supplied by Mr Finney, which, though none of them could read it, still afforded in its very touch and aspect positive corroboration of what was told them; and Jonathan Crumple pondered deeply over his (Crumple) returning wealth; and Job Skulpit saw how right he (Crumple) had been in signing the petition, and said so many scores of times; and Spriggs leered fearfully with his (Spriggs) one eye; and Moody, as he (Moody) more nearly approached the coming golden age, hated more deeply than ever those who still kept possession of what he (Moody) so coveted. Even Billy Gazy and poor bed-ridden Bell became active and uneasy, and the great Bunce stood apart with lowering brow, with deep grief seated in his (Bunce) heart, for he (Bunce) perceived that evil days were coming.

It had been decided, the archdeacon advising, that no remonstrance, explanation, or defence should be addressed from the Barchester conclave to the editor of _The Jupiter_; but hitherto that was the only decision to which they had come.

Sir Abraham Haphazard was deeply engaged in preparing a bill for the mortification of papists, to be called the

``Convent Custody Bill,'' (papists)
the purport of which was to enable any Protestant clergyman over fifty years of age to search any nun whom he (clergyman) suspected of being in possession of treasonable papers or Jesuitical symbols; and as there were to be a hundred and thirty-seven clauses in the bill, each clause containing a separate thorn for the side of the papist, and as it was known the bill would be fought inch by inch, by fifty maddened Irishmen, the due construction and adequate dovetailing of it did consume much of Sir Abraham's time. The bill had all its desired effect. Of course it never passed into law; but it so completely divided the ranks of the Irish members, who had bound themselves together to force on the ministry a bill for compelling all men to drink Irish whiskey, and all women to wear Irish poplins, that for the remainder of the session the Great Poplin and Whiskey League was utterly harmless.

Thus it happened that Sir Abraham's opinion was not at once forthcoming, and the uncertainty, the expectation, and suffering of the folk of Barchester was maintained at a high pitch.

He (Henry) appeared once before a large circle as Luther the reformer, and delighted them with the perfect manner in which he (Luther) assumed the character; and within three days he (Luther) again astonished them by acting the part of a Capuchin friar to the very life. He (lad) was a most courageous lad, game to the backbone.

Those backing him (Henry) would sometimes think him (Henry) crushed by the weight of blows and faint with loss of blood, and his (Henry) friends would endeavour to withdraw him (Henry) from the contest; but no, Henry never gave in, was never weary of the battle. His (Henry) relations could not but admire his (Henry) pluck, but they sometimes were forced to regret that he (Henry) was inclined to be a bully; and those not so partial to him (Henry) as his (Henry) father was, observed with pain that, though he (Henry) could fawn to the masters and the archdeacon's friends, he (Henry) was imperious and masterful to the servants and the poor.

But perhaps Samuel was the general favourite; and dear little Soapy, as he (Samuel) was familiarly called, was as engaging a child as ever fond mother petted. He (Samuel) was soft and gentle in his (Samuel) manners, and attractive in his (Samuel) speech; the tone of his (Samuel) voice was melody, and every action was a grace; unlike his (Samuel) brothers, he (Samuel) was courteous to all, he (Samuel) was affable to the lowly, and meek even to the very scullery-maid. Whatever of submissive humility may have appeared in the gait and visage of the archdeacon during his (Samuel) colloquy with his (Samuel) wife in the sanctum of their dressing-rooms was dispelled as he (Samuel) entered his (Samuel) breakfast-parlour with erect head and powerful step. And now let us observe the well-furnished breakfast-parlour at Plumstead Episcopi, and the comfortable air of all the belongings of the rectory. Comfortable they certainly were, but neither gorgeous nor even grand; indeed, considering the money that had been spent there, the eye and taste might have been better served; there was an air of heaviness about the rooms which might have been avoided without any sacrifice of propriety; colours might have been better chosen and lights more perfectly diffused; but perhaps in doing so the thorough clerical aspect of the whole might have been somewhat marred; at any rate, it was not without ample consideration that those thick, dark, costly carpets were put down; those embossed, but sombre papers hung up; those heavy curtains draped so as to half exclude the light of the sun: nor were these old-fashioned chairs, bought at a price far exceeding that now given for more modern goods, without a purpose. The breakfast-service on the table was equally costly and equally plain; the apparent object had been to spend money without obtaining brilliancy or splendour. The urn was of thick and solid silver, as were also the tea-pot, coffee-pot, cream-ewer, and sugar-bowl; the cups were old, dim dragon china, worth about a pound a piece, but very despicable in the eyes of the uninitiated. The silver forks were so heavy as to be disagreeable to the hand, and the bread-basket was of a weight really formidable to any but robust persons. The tea consumed was the very best, the coffee the very blackest, the cream the very thickest; there was dry toast and buttered toast, muffins and crumpets; hot bread and cold bread, white bread and brown bread, home-made bread and bakers' bread, wheaten bread and oaten bread; and if there be other breads than these, they were there; there were eggs in napkins, and crispy bits of bacon under silver covers; and there were little fishes in a little box, and devilled kidneys frizzling on a hot-water dish; which, by the bye, were placed closely contiguous to the plate of the worthy archdeacon himself. Over and above this, on a snow-white napkin, spread upon the sideboard, was a huge ham and a huge sirloin; the latter having laden the dinner table on the previous evening. Such was the ordinary fare at Plumstead Episcopi.

And yet I have never found the rectory a pleasant house. The fact that man shall not live by bread alone seemed to be somewhat forgotten; and noble as was the appearance of the host, and sweet and good-natured as was the face of the hostess, talented as were the children, and excellent as were the viands and the wines, in spite of these attractions, I generally found the rectory somewhat dull. THE CONFERENCE

``It is not to be supposed but that much pain will spring out of this unnecessarily raised question. (the archdeacon)

Chapter X

TRIBULATION

Mr Harding was a sadder man than he (Harding) had ever yet been when he (Harding) returned to his (Harding) own house. He (Harding) had been wretched enough on that well-remembered morning when he (Harding) was forced to expose before his (Harding) son-in-law the publisher's account for ushering into the world his (Harding) dear book of sacred music: when after making such payments as he (Harding) could do unassisted, he (Harding) found that he (Harding) was a debtor of more than three hundred pounds; but his (Harding) sufferings then were as nothing to his (Harding) present misery;--then he (Harding) had done wrong, and he (Harding) knew it, and was able to resolve that he (Harding) would not sin in like manner again; but now he (Harding) could make no resolution, and comfort himself by no promises of firmness. He (Harding) had been forced to think that his (Harding) lot had placed him (Harding) in a false position, and he (Harding) was about to maintain that position against the opinion of the world and against his (Harding) own convictions.

He (Harding) had read with pity, amounting almost to horror, the strictures which had appeared from time to time against the Earl of Guildford as master of St Cross, and the invectives that had been heaped on rich diocesan dignitaries and overgrown sinecure pluralists. In judging of them, he (Cross) judged leniently; the whole bias of his (Cross) profession had taught him (Cross) to think that they were more sinned against than sinning, and that the animosity with which they had been pursued was venomous and unjust; but he (Cross) had not the less regarded their plight as most miserable. His (Cross) hair had stood on end and his (Cross) flesh had crept as he (Cross) read the things which had been written; he (Cross) had wondered how men could live under such a load of disgrace; how they could face their fellow-creatures while their names were bandied about so injuriously and so publicly;--and now this lot was to be his (Cross),--he (Cross), that shy, retiring man, who had so comforted himself in the hidden obscurity of his (Cross) lot, who had so enjoyed the unassuming warmth of his (Cross) own little corner,--he (Cross) was now dragged forth into the glaring day, and gibbeted before ferocious multitudes. He (Cross) entered his (Cross) own house a crestfallen, humiliated man, without a hope of overcoming the wretchedness which affected him (Cross).

He (Cross) wandered into the drawing-room where was his (Cross) daughter; but he (Cross) could not speak to her (daughter) now, so he (Cross) left it, and went into the book-room. He (Cross) was not quick enough to escape Eleanor's glance, or to prevent her (Eleanor) from seeing that he (Cross) was disturbed; and in a little while she (Eleanor) followed him (Cross). (she)
