Chapter XX

FAREWELL

On the morning after Mr Harding's return home he (Harding) received a note from the bishop full of affection, condolence, and praise.

``Pray come to me at once,'' (the bishop)
wrote the bishop,
``that we may see what had better be done; as to the hospital, I will not say a word to dissuade you; but I don't like your going to Crabtree: at any rate, come to me at once.'' (the bishop)

Mr Harding did go to him (Harding) at once; and long and confidential was the consultation between the two old friends. There they sat together the whole long day, plotting to get the better of the archdeacon, and to carry out little schemes of their own, which they knew would be opposed by the whole weight of his (Harding) authority.

The bishop's first idea was, that Mr Harding, if left to himself, would certainly starve,--not in the figurative sense in which so many of our ladies and gentlemen do starve on incomes from one to five hundred a year; not that he (Harding) would be starved as regarded dress coats, port wine, and pocket-money; but that he (Harding) would positively perish of inanition for want of bread.

``How is a man to live, when he (man) gives up all his (man) income?'' (the bishop)
said the bishop to himself. And then the good-natured little man began to consider how his (man) friend might be best rescued from a death so horrid and painful.

His (man) first proposition to Mr Harding was, that they should live together at the palace. He (Harding), the bishop, positively assured Mr Harding that he (Harding) wanted another resident chaplain,--not a young working chaplain, but a steady, middle-aged chaplain; one who would dine and drink a glass of wine with him (chaplain), talk about the archdeacon, and poke the fire. The bishop did not positively name all these duties, but he (bishop) gave Mr Harding to understand that such would be the nature of the service required.

It was not without much difficulty that Mr Harding made his (Harding) friend see that this would not suit him (Harding); that he (Harding) could not throw up the bishop's preferment, and then come and hang on at the bishop's table; that he (bishop) could not allow people to say of him (bishop) that it was an easy matter to abandon his (bishop) own income, as he (bishop) was able to sponge on that of another person. He (bishop) succeeded, however, in explaining that the plan would not do, and then the bishop brought forward another which he (bishop) had in his (bishop) sleeve. He (bishop), the bishop, had in his (bishop) will left certain moneys to Mr Harding's two daughters, imagining that Mr Harding would himself want no such assistance during his (Harding) own lifetime. This legacy amounted to three thousand pounds each, duty free; and he (Harding) now pressed it as a gift on his (Harding) friend.

``The girls, you know,'' (he)
said he (Harding),
``will have it just the same when you're gone,--and they won't want it sooner;--and as for the interest during my lifetime, it isn't worth talking about. I have more than enough.'' (he)

With much difficulty and heartfelt sorrow, Mr Harding refused also this offer. No; his (Harding) wish was to support himself, however poorly,--not to be supported on the charity of anyone. It was hard to make the bishop understand this; it was hard to make him (bishop) comprehend that the only real favour he (bishop) could confer was the continuation of his (bishop) independent friendship; but at last even this was done. At any rate, thought the bishop, he (bishop) will come and dine with me from time to time, and if he (bishop) be absolutely starving I shall see it.

Touching the precentorship, the bishop was clearly of opinion that it could be held without the other situation,--an opinion from which no one differed; and it was therefore soon settled among all the parties concerned, that Mr Harding should still be the precentor of the cathedral.

On the day following Mr Harding's return, the archdeacon reached Plumstead full of Mr Cummins's scheme regarding Puddingdale and Mr Quiverful. On the very next morning he (Quiverful) drove over to Puddingdale, and obtained the full consent of the wretched clerical Priam, who was endeavouring to feed his (Quiverful) poor Hecuba and a dozen of Hectors on the small proceeds of his (Quiverful) ecclesiastical kingdom. Mr Quiverful had no doubts as to the legal rights of the warden; his (Quiverful) conscience would be quite clear as to accepting the income; and as to _The Jupiter_, he (Quiverful) begged to assure the archdeacon that he (Quiverful) was quite indifferent to any emanations from the profane portion of the periodical press.

Having so far succeeded, he (Quiverful) next sounded the bishop; but here he (Quiverful) was astonished by most unexpected resistance. The bishop did not think it would do.

``Not do, why not?'' (father)
and seeing that his (bishop) father was not shaken, he (bishop) repeated the question in a severer form:
``Why not do, my lord?'' (he)

His (bishop) lordship looked very unhappy, and shuffled about in his (bishop) chair, but still didn't give way; he (bishop) thought Puddingdale wouldn't do for Mr Harding; it was too far from Barchester.

``Oh! of course he (lord)'ll have a curate.'' (The bishop)

The bishop also thought that Mr Quiverful wouldn't do for the hospital; such an exchange wouldn't look well at such a time; and, when pressed harder, he (Mr) declared he (Mr) didn't think Mr Harding would accept of Puddingdale under any circumstances.

``How is he (lord) to live?'' (the archdeacon)
demanded the archdeacon.

The bishop, with tears in his (bishop) eyes, declared that he (bishop) had not the slightest conception how life was to be sustained within him (bishop) at all.

The archdeacon then left his (bishop) father, and went down to the hospital; but Mr Harding wouldn't listen at all to the Puddingdale scheme. To his (Harding) eyes it had no attraction; it savoured of simony, and was likely to bring down upon him (Harding) harder and more deserved strictures than any he (Harding) had yet received: he (Harding) positively declined to become vicar of Puddingdale under any circumstances.

The archdeacon waxed wroth, talked big, and looked bigger; he (Harding) said something about dependence and beggary, spoke of the duty every man was under to earn his (man) bread, made passing allusions to the follies of youth and waywardness of age, as though Mr Harding were afflicted by both, and ended by declaring that he (Harding) had done. He (Harding) felt that he (Harding) had left no stone unturned to arrange matters on the best and easiest footing; that he (Harding) had, in fact, so arranged them, that he (Harding) had so managed that there was no further need of any anxiety in the matter. And how had he (Harding) been paid? His (Harding) advice had been systematically rejected; he (Harding) had been not only slighted, but distrusted and avoided; he (Harding) and his (Harding) measures had been utterly thrown over, as had been Sir Abraham, who, he (Abraham) had reason to know, was much pained at what had occurred. He (Abraham) now found it was useless to interfere any further, and he (Abraham) should retire. If any further assistance were required from him (Abraham), he (Abraham) would probably be called on, and should be again happy to come forward. And so he (Abraham) left the hospital, and has not since entered it from that day to this.

And here we must take leave of Archdeacon Grantly. We fear that he (Abraham) is represented in these pages as being worse than he (Abraham) is; but we have had to do with his (Abraham) foibles, and not with his (Abraham) virtues. We have seen only the weak side of the man, and have lacked the opportunity of bringing him (Abraham) forward on his (Abraham) strong ground. That he (Abraham) is a man somewhat too fond of his (Abraham) own way, and not sufficiently scrupulous in his (Abraham) manner of achieving it, his (Abraham) best friends cannot deny. That he (Abraham) is bigoted in favour, not so much of his (Abraham) doctrines as of his (Abraham) cloth, is also true: and it is true that the possession of a large income is a desire that sits near his (Abraham) heart. Nevertheless, the archdeacon is a gentleman and a man of conscience; he (man) spends his (man) money liberally, and does the work he (man) has to do with the best of his (man) ability; he (man) improves the tone of society of those among whom he (man) lives. His (man) aspirations are of a healthy, if not of the highest, kind. Though never an austere man, he (man) upholds propriety of conduct both by example and precept. He (man) is generous to the poor, and hospitable to the rich; in matters of religion he (man) is sincere, and yet no Pharisee; he (man) is in earnest, and yet no fanatic. On the whole, the Archdeacon of Barchester is a man doing more good than harm,--a man to be furthered and supported, though perhaps also to be controlled; and it is matter of regret to us that the course of our narrative has required that we should see more of his (man) weakness than his (man) strength.

Mr Harding allowed himself no rest till everything was prepared for his (Harding) departure from the hospital. It may be as well to mention that he (Harding) was not driven to the stern necessity of selling all his (Harding) furniture: he (Harding) had been quite in earnest in his (Harding) intention to do so, but it was soon made known to him (Harding) that the claims of Messrs Cox and Cummins made no such step obligatory. The archdeacon had thought it wise to make use of the threat of the lawyer's bill, to frighten his (lawyer) father-in-law into compliance; but he (lawyer) had no intention to saddle Mr Harding with costs, which had been incurred by no means exclusively for his (lawyer) benefit. The amount of the bill was added to the diocesan account, and was, in fact, paid out of the bishop's pocket, without any consciousness on the part of his (bishop) lordship. A great part of his (bishop) furniture he (bishop) did resolve to sell, having no other means to dispose of it; and the ponies and carriage were transferred, by private contract, to the use of an old maiden lady in the city.

For his (bishop) present use Mr Harding took a lodging in Barchester, and thither were conveyed such articles as he (bishop) wanted for daily use:--his (bishop) music, books, and instruments, his (bishop) own arm-chair, and Eleanor's pet sofa; her (Eleanor) teapoy and his (bishop) cellaret, and also the slender but still sufficient contents of his (bishop) wine-cellar. Mrs Grantly had much wished that her (Grantly) sister would reside at Plumstead, till her (Grantly) father's house at Crabtree should be ready for her (Grantly); but Eleanor herself strongly resisted this proposal. It was in vain urged upon her (Grantly), that a lady in lodgings cost more than a gentleman; and that, under her (lady) father's present circumstances, such an expense should be avoided. Eleanor had not pressed her (Eleanor) father to give up the hospital in order that she (Eleanor) might live at Plumstead Rectory and he (father) alone in his (father) Barchester lodgings; nor did Eleanor think that she (Eleanor) would be treating a certain gentleman very fairly, if she (Eleanor) betook herself to the house which he (gentleman) would be the least desirous of entering of any in the county. So she (Eleanor) got a little bedroom for herself behind the sitting-room, and just over the little back parlour of the chemist, with whom they were to lodge. There was somewhat of a savour of senna softened by peppermint about the place; but, on the whole, the lodgings were clean and comfortable.

The day had been fixed for the migration of the ex-warden, and all Barchester were in a state of excitement on the subject. Opinion was much divided as to the propriety of Mr Harding's conduct. The mercantile part of the community, the mayor and corporation, and council, also most of the ladies, were loud in his (mayor) praise. Nothing could be more noble, nothing more generous, nothing more upright. But the gentry were of a different way of thinking,--especially the lawyers and the clergymen. They said such conduct was very weak and undignified; that Mr Harding evinced a lamentable want of _esprit de corps_, as well as courage; and that such an abdication must do much harm, and could do but little good.

On the evening before he (Harding) left, he (Harding) summoned all the bedesmen into his (Harding) parlour to wish them good-bye. With Bunce he (Bunce) had been in frequent communication since his (Bunce) return from London, and had been at much pains to explain to the old man the cause of his (man) resignation, without in any way prejudicing the position of his (man) successor. The others, also, he (man) had seen more or less frequently; and had heard from most of them separately some expression of regret at his (man) departure; but he (man) had postponed his (man) farewell till the last evening.

He (man) now bade the maid put wine and glasses on the table; and had the chairs arranged around the room; and sent Bunce to each of the men to request they would come and say farewell to their late warden. Soon the noise of aged scuffling feet was heard upon the gravel and in the little hall, and the eleven men who were enabled to leave their rooms were assembled.

``Come in, my friends, come in,'' (the warden)
said the warden;--he (warden) was still warden then.
``Come in, and sit down;'' (he)
and he (warden) took the hand of Abel Handy, who was the nearest to him (Handy), and led the limping grumbler to a chair. The others followed slowly and bashfully; the infirm, the lame, and the blind: poor wretches! who had been so happy, had they but known it! Now their aged faces were covered with shame, and every kind word from their master was a coal of fire burning on their heads.

When first the news had reached them that Mr Harding was going to leave the hospital, it had been received with a kind of triumph;--his (Harding) departure was, as it were, a prelude to success. He (Harding) had admitted his (Harding) want of right to the money about which they were disputing; and as it did not belong to him (Harding), of course, it did to them. The one hundred a year to each of them was actually becoming a reality; and Abel Handy was a hero, and Bunce a faint-hearted sycophant, worthy neither honour nor fellowship. But other tidings soon made their way into the old men's rooms. It was first notified to them that the income abandoned by Mr Harding would not come to them; and these accounts were confirmed by attorney Finney. They were then informed that Mr Harding's place would be at once filled by another. That the new warden could not be a kinder man they all knew; that he (warden) would be a less friendly one most suspected; and then came the bitter information that, from the moment of Mr Harding's departure, the twopence a day, his (Harding) own peculiar gift, must of necessity be withdrawn.

And this was to be the end of all their mighty struggle,--of their fight for their rights,--of their petition, and their debates, and their hopes! They were to change the best of masters for a possible bad one, and to lose twopence a day each man! No; unfortunate as this was, it was not the worst, or nearly the worst, as will just now be seen.

``Sit down, sit down, my friends,'' (the warden)
said the warden;
``I want to say a word to you and to drink your healths, before I leave you. Come up here, Moody, here is a chair for you; come, Jonathan Crumple;'' (the warden)
--and by degrees he (warden) got the men to be seated. It was not surprising that they should hang back with faint hearts, having returned so much kindness with such deep ingratitude. Last of all of them came Bunce, and with sorrowful mien and slow step got into his (Bunce) accustomed seat near the fire-place.

When they were all in their places, Mr Harding rose to address them; and then finding himself not quite at home on his (Harding) legs, he (Harding) sat down again.

``My dear old friends,'' (he)
said he (Harding),
``you all know that I am going to leave you.'' (he)

There was a sort of murmur ran round the room, intended, perhaps, to express regret at his (Harding) departure; but it was but a murmur, and might have meant that or anything else.

``There has been lately some misunderstanding between us. You have thought, I believe, that you did not get all that you were entitled to, and that the funds of the hospital have not been properly disposed of. As for me, I cannot say what should be the disposition of these moneys, or how they should be managed, and I have therefore thought it best to go.'' (unknown)

``We never wanted to drive your reverence out of it,'' (unknown)
said Handy.

``No, indeed, your reverence,'' (Skulpit/Job Skulpit)
said Skulpit.
``We never thought it would come to this. When I signed the petition,--that is, I didn't sign it, because--'' (unknown)

``Let his (me) reverence speak, can't you?'' (Moody)
said Moody.

``No,'' (Mr Harding/Harding)
continued Mr Harding;
``I am sure you did not wish to turn me out; but I thought it best to leave you. I am not a very good hand at a lawsuit, as you may all guess; and when it seemed necessary that our ordinary quiet mode of living should be disturbed, I thought it better to go. I am neither angry nor offended with any man in the hospital.'' (Mr Harding/Harding)

Here Bunce uttered a kind of groan, very clearly expressive of disagreement.

``I am neither angry nor displeased with any man in the hospital,'' (Mr Harding/Harding)
repeated Mr Harding, emphatically.
``If any man has been wrong,--and I don't say any man has,--he (man) has erred through wrong advice. In this country all are entitled to look for their own rights, and you have done no more. As long as your interests and my interests were at variance, I could give you no counsel on this subject; but the connection between us has ceased; my income can no longer depend on your doings, and therefore, as I leave you, I venture to offer to you my advice.'' (The men)

The men all declared that they would from henceforth be entirely guided by Mr Harding's opinion in their affairs.

``Some gentleman will probably take my place here very soon, and I strongly advise you to be prepared to receive him (gentleman) in a kindly spirit and to raise no further question among yourselves as to the amount of his (gentleman) income. Were you to succeed in lessening what he (gentleman) has to receive, you would not increase your own allowance. The surplus would not go to you; your wants are adequately provided for, and your position could hardly be improved.'' (unknown)

``God bless your reverence, we knows it,'' (Spriggs)
said Spriggs.

``It's all true, your reverence,'' (Skulpit/Job Skulpit)
said Skulpit.
``We sees it all now.'' (unknown)

``Yes, Mr Harding,'' (Bunce/Mr Bunce)
said Bunce, opening his (Bunce) mouth for the first time;
``I believe they do understand it now, now that they've driven from under the same roof with them such a master as not one of them will ever know again,--now that they're like to be in sore want of a friend.'' (Bunce/Mr Bunce)

``Come, come, Bunce,'' (Mr Harding/Harding)
said Mr Harding, blowing his (Harding) nose and manoeuvring to wipe his (Harding) eyes at the same time.

``Oh, as to that,'' (unknown)
said Handy,
``we none of us never wanted to do Mr Harding no harm; if he (Harding)'s going now, it's not along of us; and I don't see for what Mr Bunce speaks up agen us that way.'' (unknown)

``You've ruined yourselves, and you've ruined me too, and that's why,'' (Bunce/Mr Bunce)
said Bunce.

``Nonsense, Bunce,'' (Mr Harding/Harding)
said Mr Harding;
``there's nobody ruined at all. I hope you'll let me leave you all friends; I hope you'll all drink a glass of wine in friendly feeling with me and with one another. You'll have a good friend, I don't doubt, in your new warden; and if ever you want any other, why after all I'm not going so far off but that I shall sometimes see you;'' (Mr Harding/Harding)
and then, having finished his (Harding) speech, Mr Harding filled all the glasses, and himself handed each a glass to the men round him (Harding), and raising his (Harding) own said:--

``God bless you all! you have my heartfelt wishes for your welfare. I hope you may live contented, and die trusting in the Lord Jesus Christ, and thankful to Almighty God for the good things he (God) has given you. God bless you, my friends!'' (him)
and Mr Harding drank his (Harding) wine.

Another murmur, somewhat more articulate than the first, passed round the circle, and this time it was intended to imply a blessing on Mr Harding. It had, however, but little cordiality in it. Poor old men! how could they be cordial with their sore consciences and shamed faces? how could they bid God bless him (God) with hearty voices and a true benison, knowing, as they did, that their vile cabal had driven him (God) from his (God) happy home, and sent him (God) in his (God) old age to seek shelter under a strange roof-tree? They did their best, however; they drank their wine, and withdrew.

As they left the hall-door, Mr Harding shook hands with each of the men, and spoke a kind word to them about their individual cases and ailments; and so they departed, answering his (Harding) questions in the fewest words, and retreated to their dens, a sorrowful repentant crew.

All but Bunce, who still remained to make his (Bunce) own farewell.

``There's poor old Bell,'' (Mr Harding/Harding)
said Mr Harding;
``I mustn't go without saying a word to him (Bell); come through with me, Bunce, and bring the wine with you;'' (Mr Harding/Harding)
and so they went through to the men's cottages, and found the old man propped up as usual in his (Harding) bed.

``I've come to say good-bye to you, Bell,'' (Mr Harding/Harding)
said Mr Harding, speaking loud, for the old man was deaf.

``And are you going away, then, really?'' (Bell/Johnny Bell)
asked Bell.

``Indeed I am, and I've brought you a glass of wine; so that we may part friends, as we lived, you know.'' (The man)

The old man took the proffered glass in his (man) shaking hands, and drank it eagerly.

``God bless you, Bell!'' (Mr Harding/Harding)
said Mr Harding;
``good-bye, my old friend.'' (Mr Harding/Harding)

``And so you're really going?'' (the man)
the man again asked.

``Indeed I am, Bell.'' (The creature)

The poor old bed-ridden creature still kept Mr Harding's hand in his (Harding) own, and the warden thought that he (Harding) had met with something like warmth of feeling in the one of all his (Harding) subjects from whom it was the least likely to be expected; for poor old Bell had nearly outlived all human feelings.

``And your reverence,'' (he)
said he (Bell), and then he (Bell) paused, while his (Bell) old palsied head shook horribly, and his (Bell) shrivelled cheeks sank lower within his (Bell) jaws, and his (Bell) glazy eye gleamed with a momentary light;
``and your reverence, shall we get the hundred a year, then?'' (he)

How gently did Mr Harding try to extinguish the false hope of money which had been so wretchedly raised to disturb the quiet of the dying man! One other week and his (man) mortal coil would be shuffled off; in one short week would God resume his (God) soul, and set it apart for its irrevocable doom; seven more tedious days and nights of senseless inactivity, and all would be over for poor Bell in this world; and yet, with his (Bell) last audible words, he (Bell) was demanding his (Bell) moneyed rights, and asserting himself to be the proper heir of John Hiram's bounty! Not on him (Hiram), poor sinner as he (Hiram) was, be the load of such sin!

Mr Harding returned to his (Harding) parlour, meditating with a sick heart on what he (Harding) had seen, and Bunce with him (Harding). We will not describe the parting of these two good men, for good men they were. It was in vain that the late warden endeavoured to comfort the heart of the old bedesman; poor old Bunce felt that his (Bunce) days of comfort were gone. The hospital had to him (Bunce) been a happy home, but it could be so no longer. He (Bunce) had had honour there, and friendship; he (Bunce) had recognised his (Bunce) master, and been recognised; all his (Bunce) wants, both of soul and body, had been supplied, and he (Bunce) had been a happy man. He (Bunce) wept grievously as he (Bunce) parted from his (Bunce) friend, and the tears of an old man are bitter.

``It is all over for me in this world,'' (he)
said he (man), as he (man) gave the last squeeze to Mr Harding's hand;
``I have now to forgive those who have injured me;--and to die.'' (Mr Harding/Harding)

And so the old man went out, and then Mr Harding gave way to his (Harding) grief and he (Harding) too wept aloud.

Chapter XXI

CONCLUSION

Our tale is now done, and it only remains to us to collect the scattered threads of our little story, and to tie them into a seemly knot. This will not be a work of labour, either to the author or to his (author) readers; we have not to deal with many personages, or with stirring events, and were it not for the custom of the thing, we might leave it to the imagination of all concerned to conceive how affairs at Barchester arranged themselves.

On the morning after the day last alluded to, Mr Harding, at an early hour, walked out of the hospital, with his (Harding) daughter under his (Harding) arm, and sat down quietly to breakfast at his (Harding) lodgings over the chemist's shop. There was no parade about his (Harding) departure; no one, not even Bunce, was there to witness it; had he (Bunce) walked to the apothecary's thus early to get a piece of court plaster, or a box of lozenges, he (Bunce) could not have done it with less appearance of an important movement. There was a tear in Eleanor's eye as she (Eleanor) passed through the big gateway and over the bridge; but Mr Harding walked with an elastic step, and entered his (Harding) new abode with a pleasant face.

``Now, my dear,'' (he)
said he (Harding),
``you have everything ready, and you can make tea here just as nicely as in the parlour at the hospital.'' (he)
So Eleanor took off her (Eleanor) bonnet and made the tea. After this manner did the late Warden of Barchester Hospital accomplish his (Warden) flitting, and change his (Warden) residence.

It was not long before the archdeacon brought his (Warden) father to discuss the subject of a new warden. Of course he (Warden) looked upon the nomination as his (Warden) own, and he (Warden) had in his (Warden) eye three or four fitting candidates, seeing that Mr Cummins's plan as to the living of Puddingdale could not be brought to bear. How can I describe the astonishment which confounded him (Cummins), when his (Cummins) father declared that he (Cummins) would appoint no successor to Mr Harding?

``If we can get the matter set to rights, Mr Harding will return,'' (the bishop)
said the bishop;
``and if we cannot, it will be wrong to put any other gentleman into so cruel a position.'' (the bishop)

It was in vain that the archdeacon argued and lectured, and even threatened; in vain he (Harding) my-lorded his (Harding) poor father in his (Harding) sternest manner; in vain his (Harding)

``good heavens!'' (father)
were ejaculated in a tone that might have moved a whole synod, let alone one weak and aged bishop. Nothing could induce his (bishop) father to fill up the vacancy caused by Mr Harding's retirement.

Even John Bold would have pitied the feelings with which the archdeacon returned to Plumstead: the church was falling, nay, already in ruins; its dignitaries were yielding without a struggle before the blows of its antagonists; and one of its most respected bishops, his (Harding) own father,--the man considered by all the world as being in such matters under his (Harding), Dr Grantly's, control,--had positively resolved to capitulate, and own himself vanquished!

And how fared the hospital under this resolve of its visitor? Badly indeed. It is now some years since Mr Harding left it, and the warden's house is still tenantless. Old Bell has died, and Billy Gazy; the one-eyed Spriggs has drunk himself to death, and three others of the twelve have been gathered into the churchyard mould. Six have gone, and the six vacancies remain unfilled! Yes, six have died, with no kind friend to solace their last moments, with no wealthy neighbour to administer comforts and ease the stings of death. Mr Harding, indeed, did not desert them; from him (Harding) they had such consolation as a dying man may receive from his (man) Christian pastor; but it was the occasional kindness of a stranger which ministered to them, and not the constant presence of a master, a neighbour, and a friend.

Nor were those who remained better off than those who died. Dissensions rose among them, and contests for pre-eminence; and then they began to understand that soon one among them would be the last,--some one wretched being would be alone there in that now comfortless hospital,--the miserable relic of what had once been so good and so comfortable.

The building of the hospital itself has not been allowed to go to ruins. Mr Chadwick, who still holds his (Chadwick) stewardship, and pays the accruing rents into an account opened at a bank for the purpose, sees to that; but the whole place has become disordered and ugly. The warden's garden is a wretched wilderness, the drive and paths are covered with weeds, the flower-beds are bare, and the unshorn lawn is now a mass of long damp grass and unwholesome moss. The beauty of the place is gone; its attractions have withered. Alas! a very few years since it was the prettiest spot in Barchester, and now it is a disgrace to the city.

Mr Harding did not go out to Crabtree Parva. An arrangement was made which respected the homestead of Mr Smith and his (Mr) happy family, and put Mr Harding into possession of a small living within the walls of the city. It is the smallest possible parish, containing a part of the Cathedral Close and a few old houses adjoining. The church is a singular little Gothic building, perched over a gateway, through which the Close is entered, and is approached by a flight of stone steps which leads down under the archway of the gate. It is no bigger than an ordinary room,--perhaps twenty-seven feet long by eighteen wide,--but still it is a perfect church. It contains an old carved pulpit and reading-desk, a tiny altar under a window filled with dark old-coloured glass, a font, some half-dozen pews, and perhaps a dozen seats for the poor; and also a vestry. The roof is high pitched, and of black old oak, and the three large beams which support it run down to the side walls, and terminate in grotesquely carved faces,--two devils and an angel on one side, two angels and a devil on the other. Such is the church of St Cuthbert at Barchester, of which Mr Harding became rector, with a clear income of seventy-five pounds a year.

Here he (Harding) performs afternoon service every Sunday, and administers the Sacrament once in every three months. His (Harding) audience is not large; and, had they been so, he (Harding) could not have accommodated them: but enough come to fill his (Harding) six pews, and on the front seat of those devoted to the poor is always to be seen our old friend Mr Bunce, decently arrayed in his (Bunce) bedesman's gown.

Mr Harding is still precentor of Barchester; and it is very rarely the case that those who attend the Sunday morning service miss the gratification of hearing him (Harding) chant the Litany, as no other man in England can do it. He (man) is neither a discontented nor an unhappy man; he (man) still inhabits the lodgings to which he (man) went on leaving the hospital, but he (man) now has them to himself. Three months after that time Eleanor became Mrs Bold, and of course removed to her (Mrs) husband's house.

There were some difficulties to be got over on the occasion of the marriage. The archdeacon, who could not so soon overcome his (husband) grief, would not be persuaded to grace the ceremony with his (husband) presence, but he (husband) allowed his (husband) wife and children to be there. The marriage took place in the cathedral, and the bishop himself officiated. It was the last occasion on which he (bishop) ever did so; and, though he (bishop) still lives, it is not probable that he (bishop) will ever do so again.

Not long after the marriage, perhaps six months, when Eleanor's bridal-honours were fading, and persons were beginning to call her (Eleanor) Mrs Bold without twittering, the archdeacon consented to meet John Bold at a dinner-party, and since that time they have become almost friends. The archdeacon firmly believes that his (Bold) brother-in-law was, as a bachelor, an infidel, an unbeliever in the great truths of our religion; but that matrimony has opened his (Bold) eyes, as it has those of others. And Bold is equally inclined to think that time has softened the asperities of the archdeacon's character. Friends though they are, they do not often revert to the feud of the hospital.

Mr Harding, we say, is not an unhappy man: he (Harding) keeps his (Harding) lodgings, but they are of little use to him (Harding), except as being the one spot on earth which he (Harding) calls his (Harding) own. His (Harding) time is spent chiefly at his (Harding) daughter's or at the palace; he (Harding) is never left alone, even should he (Harding) wish to be so; and within a twelvemonth of Eleanor's marriage his (Harding) determination to live at his (Harding) own lodging had been so far broken through and abandoned, that he (Harding) consented to have his (Harding) violoncello permanently removed to his (Harding) daughter's house.

Every other day a message is brought to him (Harding) from the bishop.

``The bishop's compliments, and his (bishop) lordship is not very well to-day, and he (bishop) hopes Mr Harding will dine with him (Harding).'' (the man)
This bulletin as to the old man's health is a myth; for though he (man) is over eighty he (man) is never ill, and will probably die some day, as a spark goes out, gradually and without a struggle. Mr Harding does dine with him (Harding) very often, which means going to the palace at three and remaining till ten; and whenever he (Harding) does not the bishop whines, and says that the port wine is corked, and complains that nobody attends to him (Harding), and frets himself off to bed an hour before his (Harding) time.

It was long before the people of Barchester forgot to call Mr Harding by his (Harding) long well-known name of Warden. It had become so customary to say Mr Warden, that it was not easily dropped.

``No, no,'' (he)
he (Warden) always says when so addressed,
``not warden now, only precentor.'' (he)
